Russian agricultural slaves were formally converted into serfs earlier in 1679.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>'s more than 23 million privately held <a href="/wiki/Serfdom_in_Russia" title="Serfdom in Russia">serfs</a> were freed by the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_reform_of_1861" title="Emancipation reform of 1861">Emancipation reform of 1861</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slavery_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-slavery-163">[163]</a></sup> State-owned serfs were emancipated in 1866.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until the late 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a> (a Muslim Tatar state) maintained a massive slave trade with the Ottoman Empire and the Middle East,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slave_trade_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-slave_trade-146">[146]</a></sup> exporting about 2 million slaves from Poland-Lithuania and Russia over the period 1500–1700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> (1939–1945) <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour_under_German_rule_during_World_War_II" title="Forced labour under German rule during World War II">Nazi Germany effectively enslaved about 12 million people</a>, both those considered undesirable and citizens of countries they conquered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa_3">Africa</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Africa" title="Slavery in Africa">Slavery in Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_slave_trade.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="721" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/African_slave_trade.png/220px-African_slave_trade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/African_slave_trade.png/330px-African_slave_trade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/African_slave_trade.png/440px-African_slave_trade.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_slave_trade.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The main routes that were used to transport slaves across medieval Africa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>, the capital of <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, captured <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europeans</a> were forced into slavery. This eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Bombardment_of_Algiers_(1816)" title="Bombardment of Algiers (1816)">bombardment of Algiers</a> by an Anglo-Dutch fleet in 1816.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3585" data-file-width="2531" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg/170px-Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg/255px-Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg/340px-Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slave traders in <a href="/wiki/Gor%C3%A9e" title="Gorée">Gorée</a>, Senegal, 18th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Half the population of the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto" title="Sokoto">Sokoto</a> caliphate of the 19th century (based in the future northern Nigeria) were slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup> The Swahili-Arab slave trade reached its height about 160 years ago, when, for example, approximately 20,000 slaves were considered to be carried yearly from <a href="/wiki/Nkhotakota" title="Nkhotakota">Nkhotakota</a> on Lake Malawi to Kilwa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Roughly half the population of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> was enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_African_History" title="Encyclopedia of African History">Encyclopedia of African History</a></i>, "It is estimated that by the 1890s the largest slave population of the world, about 2 million people, was concentrated in the territories of the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a>. The use of slave labour was extensive, especially in agriculture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The Anti-Slavery Society estimated there were 2 million slaves in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in the early 1930s out of an estimated population of 8 to 16 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twentieth1_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-twentieth1-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="1000" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/220px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/330px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/440px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arab slave traders and their captives along the Ruvuma river (in today's Tanzania and Mozambique).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Clapperton" title="Hugh Clapperton">Hugh Clapperton</a> in 1824 believed that half the population of <a href="/wiki/Kano" title="Kano">Kano</a> were enslaved people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> W. A. Veenhoven wrote: "The German doctor, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Nachtigal" title="Gustav Nachtigal">Gustav Nachtigal</a>, an eye-witness, believed that for every slave who arrived at a market three or four died on the way ... <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Keltie" title="John Scott Keltie">Keltie</a> (<i>The Partition of Africa</i>, London, 1920) believes that for every slave the Arabs brought to the coast at least six died on the way or during the slavers' raid. <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">Livingstone</a> puts the figure as high as ten to one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most famous slave traders on the eastern <a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">Zanj</a> (Bantu) coast was <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>, himself the grandson of a slave. The <i>prazeros</i> were slave-traders along the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a>. North of the Zambezi, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WaYao" title="WaYao">waYao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makua_people" title="Makua people">Makua people</a> played a similar role as professional slave-raiders and -traders. Still further north were the <a href="/wiki/Nyamwezi_people" title="Nyamwezi people">Nyamwezi</a> slave-traders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_2">Asia</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Asia" title="History of slavery in Asia">History of slavery in Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="903" data-file-width="600" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/170px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/255px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/340px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A contract from the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> that records the purchase of a 15-year-old slave for six bolts of plain silk and five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_coins" title="Chinese coins">Chinese coins</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, about one-fifth of the population consisted of slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup> The city was a major centre of the slave trade in the 15th and later centuries. By 1475 most of the slaves were provided by Tatar raids on Slavic villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> It has been estimated that some 200,000 slaves—mainly <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>—were imported into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> between 1800 and 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abolition_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-178">[178]</a></sup> As late as 1908, women slaves were still sold in the Ottoman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> A slave market for captured <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> slaves was centred in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Khiva" title="Khanate of Khiva">khanate of Khiva</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> In the early 1840s, the population of the Uzbek states of <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Bukhara" title="Emirate of Bukhara">Bukhara</a> and Khiva included about 900,000 slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abolition_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-178">[178]</a></sup> Darrel P. Kaiser wrote, "<a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakh</a>-<a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kirghiz</a> tribesmen kidnapped 1573 settlers from colonies [German settlements in Russia] in 1774 alone and only half were successfully ransomed. The rest were killed or enslaved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bartle_Frere" title="Henry Bartle Frere">Henry Bartle Frere</a> (who sat on the Viceroy's Council), there were an estimated 8 or 9 million slaves in <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a> in 1841. About 15% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Malabar_District" title="Malabar District">Malabar</a> were slaves. Slavery was abolished in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_India" title="Slavery in India">Indian Slavery</a> Act V. of 1843.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Imperial</a> government formally abolished slavery in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in 1906, and the law became effective in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Social_classes_of_Tibet" title="Social classes of Tibet">Nangzan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibetan</a> history were, according to Chinese sources, hereditary household slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indigenous slaves existed in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>. Slavery was abolished with the <a href="/wiki/Gabo_Reform" title="Gabo Reform">Gabo Reform</a> of 1894, but continued in reality until 1930. During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseon_Dynasty" title="Joseon Dynasty">Joseon Dynasty</a> (1392–1910), about 30% to 50% of the <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Korean</a> population were slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup> In late 16th century <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, slavery as such was officially banned, but forms of contract and indentured labour persisted alongside the period penal codes' forced labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hill_tribe#South-East_Asia" title="Hill tribe">hill tribe</a> people in <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> were "hunted incessantly and carried off as slaves by the Siamese (Thai), the Anamites (Vietnamese), and the Cambodians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> A Siamese military campaign in Laos in 1876 was described by a British observer as having been "transformed into slave-hunting raids on a large scale".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The census, taken in 1879, showed that 6% of the population in the <a href="/wiki/Monarchies_of_Malaysia" title="Monarchies of Malaysia">Malay</a> sultanate of <a href="/wiki/Perak" title="Perak">Perak</a> were slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abolition_178-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-178">[178]</a></sup> Enslaved people made up about two-thirds of the population in part of <a href="/wiki/North_Borneo" title="North Borneo">North Borneo</a> in the 1880s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abolition_178-3"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the 1930s and 1940s the <a href="/wiki/Yi_people" title="Yi people">Yi people</a> (also known as Nuosu) of China terrorized Sichuan to rob and enslave non-Nuosu including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_people" title="Han people">Han people</a>. The descendants of the Han Chinese slaves are the White Yi (白彝) and they outnumber the Black Yi (黑彝) aristocracy by ten to one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> As much as tens of thousands of Han slaves were incorporated into Nuosu society every year. The Han slaves and their offspring were used for manual labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> There is a saying goes like: "the worst insult to a Nuosu is to call him a "Han" (with the implication being that "your ancestors were slaves")".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">Encomienda</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mita_(Inca)" title="Mita (Inca)">Mita (Inca)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Brazil" title="Slavery in Brazil">Slavery in Brazil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">Slavery in the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Capitao-mato.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="303" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Capitao-mato.jpg/170px-Capitao-mato.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Capitao-mato.jpg/255px-Capitao-mato.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Capitao-mato.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Capitao-mato.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Brazilian slave-hunter, 1823, by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Moritz_Rugendas" title="Johann Moritz Rugendas">Johann Moritz Rugendas</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slavery in the Americas had a contentious history, and played a major role in the history and evolution of some countries, triggering at least <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">one revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">one civil war</a>, as well as numerous rebellions. The <a href="/wiki/Aztec_slavery" title="Aztec slavery">Aztecs</a> had slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Other Amerindians, such as the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca</a> of the Andes, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tup%C3%AD_people" title="Tupí people">Tupinambá</a> of Brazil, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscogee_people" title="Muscogee people">Creek</a> of Georgia, and the <a href="/wiki/Comanche" title="Comanche">Comanche</a> of Texas, also owned slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The maritime town of <a href="/wiki/Lagos,_Portugal" title="Lagos, Portugal">Lagos</a> was the first slave market created in Portugal (one of the earliest colonizers of the Americas) for the sale of imported African slaves—the <i>Mercado de Escravos</i>, opened in 1444.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliveira_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-192">[192]</a></sup> In 1441, the first slaves were brought to Portugal from northern <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliveira_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1519, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>'s first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Mexican#African_slavery_in_Mexico" title="Afro-Mexican">Afro-Mexican</a> slave was brought by <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a>.</p>
<p>By 1552, <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black African</a> slaves made up 10% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> In the second half of the 16th century, the Crown gave up the monopoly on slave trade and the focus of European trade in African slaves shifted from import to Europe to slave transports directly to tropical colonies in the Americas—in the case of Portugal, especially <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliveira_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-192">[192]</a></sup> In the 15th century one-third of the slaves were resold to the African market in exchange of gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>In order to establish itself as an American empire, Spain had to fight against the relatively powerful civilizations of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> conquest of the indigenous peoples in the Americas included using the Natives as forced labour. The <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Spanish_New_World_colonies" title="Slavery in the Spanish New World colonies">Spanish colonies</a> were the first Europeans to use African slaves in the New World on islands such as <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelourinho.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="249" data-file-width="392" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pelourinho.jpg/220px-Pelourinho.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pelourinho.jpg/330px-Pelourinho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/41/Pelourinho.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pelourinho.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The public flogging of a slave in <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, Brazil. From Jean Baptiste Debret, <i>Voyage Pittoresque et Historique au Brésil</i> (1834–1839).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_Las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de Las Casas">Bartolomé de Las Casas</a> a 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> historian participated in campaigns in Cuba (at <a href="/wiki/Bayamo" title="Bayamo">Bayamo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camag%C3%BCey" title="Camagüey">Camagüey</a>) and was present at the massacre of <a href="/wiki/Hatuey" title="Hatuey">Hatuey</a>; his observation of that massacre led him to fight for a social movement away from the use of natives as slaves and towards the importation of African Blacks as slaves. Also, the alarming decline in the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">native</a> population had spurred the first royal laws protecting the native population (<a href="/wiki/Laws_of_Burgos" title="Laws of Burgos">Laws of Burgos, 1512–1513</a>).</p>
<p>The first African slaves arrived in Hispaniola in 1501.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> In 1518, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles I of Spain</a> agreed to ship slaves directly from Africa. England played a prominent role in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>. The "<a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade#Atlantic_triangular_slave_trade" title="Triangular trade">slave triangle</a>" was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> and his associates. In 1640 a Virginia court sentenced <a href="/wiki/John_Punch_(slave)" title="John Punch (slave)">John Punch</a> to slavery, forcing him to serve his master, Hugh Gwyn, for the remainder of his life. This was the first legal sanctioning of slavery in the English colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> In 1655, A black man, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Johnson_(colonist)" title="Anthony Johnson (colonist)">Anthony Johnson</a> of Virginia, was granted ownership of John Casor as the result of a civil case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Henrietta Marie</i> was probably built in France sometime in the 17th century and carried a crew of about eighteen men. The ship came into English possession late in the 17th century, possibly as a <a href="/wiki/Prize_(law)" title="Prize (law)">war prize</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War" title="Nine Years' War">War of the Grand Alliance</a>. It was put to use in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>, making at least two voyages carrying Africans to slavery in the West Indies. On its first voyage, in 1697–1698, the ship carried more than 200 people from Africa that were sold as slaves in <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>. In 1699 the <i>Henrietta Marie</i> sailed from England on the first leg of the <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">triangular trade</a> route with a load of trade goods, including iron and copper bars, pewter utensils, glass beads, cloth and brandy. The ship sailed under license from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_African_Company" title="Royal African Company">Royal African Company</a> (which held a monopoly on English trade with Africa), in exchange for ten percent of the profits of the voyage. It is known to have traded for African captives at <a href="/wiki/Kalabari_Kingdom" title="Kalabari Kingdom">New Calabar</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_Coast" title="Guinea Coast">Guinea Coast</a>. The ship then sailed on the second leg of its voyage, from Africa to the West Indies, and in May 1701 landed 191 Africans for sale in <a href="/wiki/Port_Royal" title="Port Royal">Port Royal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>. The <i>Henrietta Marie</i> then loaded a cargo of sugar, cotton, <a href="/wiki/Dyewoods" title="Dyewoods">dyewoods</a> (indigo) and ginger to take back to England on the third leg of the triangular route. After leaving Port Royal on 18 May 1701, the ship headed for the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Channel" title="Yucatán Channel">Yucatán Channel</a> to pass around the western end of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> (thus avoiding the pirates infesting the passage between Cuba and <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>) and catch the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>, the preferred route for all ships leaving the Caribbean to return to Europe. A month later, the <i>Henrietta Marie</i> <a href="/wiki/Shipwreck" title="Shipwreck">wrecked</a> on New Ground Reef near the <a href="/wiki/Marquesas_Keys" title="Marquesas Keys">Marquesas Keys</a>, approximately 35 miles (56 kilometres) west of <a href="/wiki/Key_West,_Florida" title="Key West, Florida">Key West</a>. All aboard were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_(1823),_plate_III_-_BL.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1392" data-file-width="1994" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg/220px-Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg/330px-Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg/440px-Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_(1823),_plate_III_-_BL.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Planting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, British colony of <a href="/wiki/Antigua" title="Antigua">Antigua</a>, 1823</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pirates often targeted slavers. For example, the 300 ton English <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigate</a> <i>Concord</i> launched in 1710 but was captured by the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> one year later. She was modified to hold more cargo, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slaves" title="Slaves">slaves</a>, and renamed <i>La Concorde de Nantes</i>. Sailing as a <a href="/wiki/Slave_ship" title="Slave ship">slave ship</a>, she was captured by the pirate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(nautical)" title="Captain (nautical)">Captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hornigold" title="Benjamin Hornigold">Benjamin Hornigold</a> on November 28, 1717, near the island of <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a>. Hornigold turned her over to one of his men, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Teach" title="Edward Teach">Edward Teach</a> (later known as <a href="/wiki/Blackbeard" title="Blackbeard">Blackbeard</a>), and made him her captain. Teach then renamed her the <i><a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne%27s_Revenge" title="Queen Anne's Revenge">Queen Anne's Revenge</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Moore._1997_pp._31_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-D._Moore._1997_pp._31-202">[202]</a></sup> By 1750, slavery was a legal institution in all of the 13 <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">American colonies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cornerstones_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-cornerstones-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> and the profits of the slave trade and of <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">West Indian</a> plantations amounted to 5% of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Economic history of the United Kingdom">British economy</a> at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>The trans-Atlantic slave trade peaked in the late 18th century, when the largest number of slaves were captured on raiding expeditions into the interior of West Africa. These expeditions were typically carried out by <a href="/wiki/List_of_kingdoms_in_pre-colonial_Africa" title="List of kingdoms in pre-colonial Africa">African kingdoms</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_Empire" title="Ashanti Empire">Ashanti Empire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dahomey" title="Dahomey">Dahomey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Aro_Confederacy" title="Aro Confederacy">Aro Confederacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Europeans rarely entered the interior of Africa, due to fierce African resistance. The slaves were brought to coastal outposts where they were traded for goods. A significant portion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a> in North America are descended from <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muse.jhu.edu_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-muse.jhu.edu-209">[209]</a></sup> Through a series of conflicts, primarily with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulani_Jihad_States" title="Fulani Jihad States">Fulani Jihad States</a>, about half of the Senegambian Mandinka were converted to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> while as many as a third were sold into slavery to the Americas through capture in conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muse.jhu.edu_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-muse.jhu.edu-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="732" data-file-width="1149" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg/220px-SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg/330px-SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg/440px-SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SlaveDanceand_Music.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slaves on a Virginia plantation (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Plantation" title="The Old Plantation">The Old Plantation</a></i>, c. 1790).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woman-slave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="492" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Woman-slave.jpg/170px-Woman-slave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Woman-slave.jpg/255px-Woman-slave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Woman-slave.jpg/340px-Woman-slave.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woman-slave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mid 19th century portrait of an older <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> woman with her child slave servant.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An estimated 12 million Africans arrived in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> from the 16th to the 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Of these, an estimated 645,000 were brought to what is now the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. The usual estimate is that about 15% of slaves died during the voyage, with mortality rates considerably higher in Africa itself in the process of capturing and transporting indigenous peoples to the ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many Europeans who arrived in North America during the 17th and 18th centuries came under contract as indentured servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> The transformation from indentured servitude to slavery was a gradual process in Virginia. The earliest legal documentation of such a shift was in 1640 where a negro, <a href="/wiki/John_Punch_(slave)" title="John Punch (slave)">John Punch</a>, was sentenced to lifetime slavery for attempting to run away. This case also marked the disparate treatment of Africans as held by the Virginia County Court, as two white runaways received far lesser sentences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> After 1640, planters started to ignore the expiration of indentured contracts and kept their servants as slaves for life. This was demonstrated by the case Johnson v. Parker, where the court ruled that <a href="/wiki/John_Casor" title="John Casor">John Casor</a>, an indentured servant, be returned to Johnson who claimed that Casor belonged to him for his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/1860_United_States_Census" title="1860 United States Census">1860 U. S. census</a>, 393,975 individuals, representing 8% of all US families, owned 3,950,528 slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> One-third of Southern families owned slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2620" data-file-width="3467" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg/220px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg/330px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg/440px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Funeral at slave plantation, Suriname. Colored lithograph printed circa 1840–1850, digitally restored.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The largest number of slaves were shipped to <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> In the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">viceroyalty of New Granada</a>, corresponding mainly to modern <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, the free black population in 1789 was 420,000, whereas African slaves numbered only 20,000. Free blacks also outnumbered slaves in Brazil. By contrast, in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, free blacks made up only 15% in 1827; and in the French colony of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>) it was a mere 5% in 1789.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>Author Charles Rappleye argued that</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In the West Indies in particular, but also in North and South America, slavery was the engine that drove the mercantile empires of Europe. It appeared, in the eighteenth century, as universal and immutable as human nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="783" data-file-width="1163" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg/220px-Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg/330px-Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg/440px-Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lady in litter being carried by her slaves, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">province of São Paulo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, ca.1860.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the trans-Atlantic slave trade ended shortly after the American Revolution, slavery remained a central economic institution in the Southern states of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, from where slavery expanded with the westward movement of population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> Historian Peter Kolchin wrote, "By breaking up existing families and forcing slaves to relocate far from everyone and everything they knew" this migration "replicated (if on a reduced level) many of [the] horrors" of the Atlantic slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Ira_Berlin" title="Ira Berlin">Ira Berlin</a> called this forced migration the Second <a href="/wiki/Middle_Passage" title="Middle Passage">Middle Passage</a>. Characterizing it as the "central event" in the life of a slave between the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and the Civil War, Berlin wrote that whether they were uprooted themselves or simply lived in fear that they or their families would be involuntarily moved, "the massive deportation traumatized black people, both slave and free.."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1860, 500,000 slaves had grown to 4 million. As long as slavery expanded, it remained profitable and powerful and was unlikely to disappear. Although complete statistics are lacking, it is estimated that 1,000,000 slaves moved west from the <a href="/wiki/Old_South" title="Old South">Old South</a> between 1790 and 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolchin_pp._582_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kolchin_pp._582-224">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of the slaves were moved from <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/The_Carolinas" title="The Carolinas">Carolinas</a>. Michael Tadman, in a 1989 book <i>Speculators and Slaves: Masters, Traders, and Slaves in the Old South</i>, indicates that 60–70% of interregional migrations were the result of the sale of slaves. In 1820, a child in the Upper South had a 30% chance to be sold south by 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolchin_pp._582_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kolchin_pp._582-224">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico#Abolition_of_Slavery" title="African immigration to Puerto Rico">African slavery</a> was finally abolished on March 22, 1873.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dutch,_17th_century,_Black_boy_with_slave_collar,_Private_Collection.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1633" data-file-width="1317" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Dutch%2C_17th_century%2C_Black_boy_with_slave_collar%2C_Private_Collection.jpg/170px-Dutch%2C_17th_century%2C_Black_boy_with_slave_collar%2C_Private_Collection.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Dutch%2C_17th_century%2C_Black_boy_with_slave_collar%2C_Private_Collection.jpg/255px-Dutch%2C_17th_century%2C_Black_boy_with_slave_collar%2C_Private_Collection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Dutch%2C_17th_century%2C_Black_boy_with_slave_collar%2C_Private_Collection.jpg/340px-Dutch%2C_17th_century%2C_Black_boy_with_slave_collar%2C_Private_Collection.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dutch,_17th_century,_Black_boy_with_slave_collar,_Private_Collection.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Page_(occupation)" title="Page (occupation)">Page Boy</a> with slave collar, Dutch 17th-century painting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East_2">Middle East</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Slavery (Ottoman Empire)">Slavery (Ottoman Empire)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_slavery" title="Islamic views on slavery">Islamic views on slavery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_on_the_Barbary_Coast" title="Slavery on the Barbary Coast">Slavery on the Barbary Coast</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1016" data-file-width="845" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg/220px-360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg/330px-360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg/440px-360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman wars in Europe</a> resulted in many captive Christians being carried deep into Muslim territory.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Robert Davis, between 1 million and 1.25 million <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">Europeans</a> were captured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbary_corsairs" title="Barbary corsairs">Barbary pirates</a> and sold as slaves in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis2003_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis2003-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> There was also an extensive trade in Christian slaves in the Black Sea region for several centuries until the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a> was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in 1783.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crimea_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crimea-227">[227]</a></sup> In the 1570s close to 20,000 slaves a year were being sold in the Crimean port of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feodosiya" title="Feodosiya">Kaffa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> The slaves were captured in southern Russia, <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland-Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Circassia" title="Circassia">Circassia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatar</a> horsemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Some researchers estimate that altogether more than 3 million people were captured and enslaved during the time of the Crimean Khanate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persian_slave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1273" data-file-width="1925" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Persian_slave.jpg/220px-Persian_slave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Persian_slave.jpg/330px-Persian_slave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Persian_slave.jpg/440px-Persian_slave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persian_slave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Persian slave in the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Khiva" title="Khanate of Khiva">Khanate of Khiva</a>, 19th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="825" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg/220px-Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg/330px-Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg/440px-Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
British captain witnessing the miseries of the Christian slaves in Algiers, 1815</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Myths-slavery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="480" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a0/Myths-slavery.jpg/220px-Myths-slavery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a0/Myths-slavery.jpg/330px-Myths-slavery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a0/Myths-slavery.jpg/440px-Myths-slavery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Myths-slavery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Arab enslavement of the <a href="/wiki/Dinka_people" title="Dinka people">Dinka people</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a> lasted more than a millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> As recently as the early 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>'s slave population was estimated at 300,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Along with Yemen, the Saudis abolished slavery only in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-234">[234]</a></sup> Historically, slaves in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_World" title="Arab World">Arab World</a> came from many different regions, including <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> (mainly <i><a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">Zanj</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1992_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1992-235">[235]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Tartary" title="Tartary">Tartars</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> (mainly <i><a href="/wiki/Saqaliba" title="Saqaliba">Saqaliba</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soldier_Khan_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soldier_Khan-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Omani</a> Arabs <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> became <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>'s main slave port, with as many as 50,000 enslaved Africans passing through every year during the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> Some historians estimate that between 11 and 18 million African slaves crossed the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> Desert from 650 to 1900 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eduard_R%C3%BCppell" title="Eduard Rüppell">Eduard Rüppell</a> described the losses of Sudanese slaves being transported on foot to Egypt: "after the Daftardar bey's 1822 campaign in the southern Nuba mountains, nearly 40,000 slaves were captured. However, through bad treatment, disease and desert travel barely 5000 made it to Egypt.."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell2007_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbell2007-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, starting in the 8th century, also raided coastal areas around the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, and became known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbary_corsairs" title="Barbary corsairs">Barbary pirates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> It is estimated that they captured 1.25 million white slaves from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> The mortality rate was very high. For instance, plague killed a third to two-thirds of the 30,000 occupants of the slave pens in <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> in 1662.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis2003_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis2003-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
