<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Anti-Slavery_Society_Convention,_1840_by_Benjamin_Robert_Haydon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The painting of the 1840 <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_Society" title="Anti-Slavery Society">Anti-Slavery Society</a> Convention at Exeter Hall. Move your cursor to identify delegates or click the icon to enlarge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npg_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-npg-245">[245]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slavery has existed, in one form or another, through recorded <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">human history</a>—as have, in various periods, movements to free large or distinct groups of slaves.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>, who ruled the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a> from 269–232 BCE, abolished the slave trade but not slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>, which ruled China from 221 to 206 BC, abolished slavery and discouraged serfdom. However, many of its laws were overturned when the dynasty was overthrown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> Slavery was again abolished, by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a>, in China in 17 C.E but was reinstituted after his assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_Publishing_Group_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood_Publishing_Group-248">[248]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonization of the Americas</a> sparked a discussion about the right to enslave Native Americans. A prominent critic of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Spanish_New_World_colonies" title="Slavery in the Spanish New World colonies">slavery in the Spanish New World colonies</a> was <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">Bartolomé de las Casas</a>, who opposed the enslavement of Native Americans, and later also of Africans in America.</p>
<p>One of the first protests against slavery came from German and Dutch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quakers</a> in Pennsylvania in 1688.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrank_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-jrank-249">[249]</a></sup> One of the most significant milestones in the campaign to abolish slavery throughout the world occurred in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in 1772, with British judge <a href="/wiki/William_Murray,_1st_Earl_of_Mansfield" title="William Murray, 1st Earl of Mansfield">Lord Mansfield</a>, whose opinion in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somersett%27s_Case" title="Somersett's Case">Somersett's Case</a> was widely taken to have held that slavery was illegal in England. This judgement also laid down the principle that slavery contracted in other jurisdictions could not be enforced in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> In 1777, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, at the time an independent nation, became the first portion of what would become the United States to abolish slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrank_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-jrank-249">[249]</a></sup> France abolished slavery in 1794.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrank_249-2"><a href="#cite_note-jrank-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="338" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg/170px-Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg/255px-Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Joseph_Jenkins_Roberts" title="Joseph Jenkins Roberts">Joseph Jenkins Roberts</a>, born in Virginia, was the first president of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, which was founded in 1822 for freed American slaves.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>British Member of Parliament <a href="/wiki/William_Wilberforce" title="William Wilberforce">William Wilberforce</a> led the anti-slavery movement in the United Kingdom, although the groundwork was an anti-slavery essay by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clarkson" title="Thomas Clarkson">Thomas Clarkson</a>. Wilberforce was also urged by his close friend, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>, to make the issue his own, and was also given support by reformed Evangelical <a href="/wiki/John_Newton" title="John Newton">John Newton</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_1807" title="Slave Trade Act 1807">Slave Trade Act</a> was passed by the British Parliament on March 25, 1807, making the slave trade illegal throughout the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> Wilberforce also campaigned for abolition of slavery in the British Empire, which he lived to see in the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">Slavery Abolition Act 1833</a>. After the 1807 act abolishing the slave trade was passed, these campaigners switched to <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Africa" title="Blockade of Africa">encouraging other countries</a> to follow suit, notably France and the British colonies. Between 1808 and 1860, the British <a href="/wiki/West_Africa_Squadron" title="West Africa Squadron">West Africa Squadron</a> seized approximately 1,600 slave ships and freed 150,000 Africans who were aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> Action was also taken against African leaders who refused to agree to British treaties to outlaw the trade, for example against "the usurping King of <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>", deposed in 1851. Anti-slavery treaties were signed with over 50 African rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1839, the world's oldest international human rights organization, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_International" title="Anti-Slavery International">Anti-Slavery International</a>, was formed in Britain by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Sturge" title="Joseph Sturge">Joseph Sturge</a>, which campaigned to outlaw slavery in other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> There were celebrations in 2007 to commemorate the 200th anniversary of the abolition of the slave trade in the United Kingdom through the work of the British <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_Society" title="Anti-Slavery Society">Anti-Slavery Society</a>.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, abolitionist pressure produced a series of small steps towards emancipation. After January 1, 1808, the importation of slaves into the United States was prohibited,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> but not the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States#Internal_slave_trade" title="Slavery in the United States">internal slave trade</a>, nor involvement in the international slave trade externally. Legal slavery persisted; and those slaves already in the U.S. were <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">legally emancipated</a> only in 1863. Many American abolitionists took an active role in opposing slavery by supporting the <a href="/wiki/Underground_Railroad" title="Underground Railroad">Underground Railroad</a>. Violent clashes between anti-slavery and pro-slavery Americans included <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">Bleeding Kansas</a>, a series of political and armed disputes in 1854–1861 as to whether Kansas would join the United States as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_and_free_states" title="Slave and free states">slave or free state</a>. By 1860, the total number of slaves reached almost four million, and the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, beginning in 1861, led to the end of slavery in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> In 1863, Lincoln issued the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a>, which freed slaves held in the Confederate States; the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">13th Amendment to the U. S. Constitution</a> prohibited most forms of slavery throughout the country.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2581" data-file-width="1558" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg/170px-Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg/255px-Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg/340px-Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cicatrices_de_flagellation_sur_un_esclave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photographed in 1863 – Peter, aka <a href="/wiki/Gordon_(slave)" title="Gordon (slave)">Gordon</a>, a man who was enslaved in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> This famous photo was distributed by abolitionists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the case of freed slaves of the United States, many became sharecroppers and indentured servants. In this manner, some became tied to the very parcel of land into which they had been born a slave having little freedom or economic opportunity due to <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow_laws" title="Jim Crow laws">Jim Crow laws</a> which perpetuated discrimination, limited education, promoted persecution without due process and resulted in continued poverty. Fear of reprisals such as unjust incarcerations and lynchings deterred upward mobility further.</p>
<p>In the 1860s, <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>'s reports of atrocities within the <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a> in Africa stirred up the interest of the British public, reviving the flagging abolitionist movement. The Royal Navy throughout the 1870s attempted to suppress "this abominable Eastern trade", at <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> in particular. In 1905, the French abolished indigenous slavery in most of <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 10, 1948, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>, which declared freedom from slavery is an internationally recognized <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human right</a>. Article 4 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>No one shall be held in slavery or servitude; slavery and the slave trade shall be prohibited in all their forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 2014, for the first time in history, major leaders of many religions, Buddhist, Anglican, Catholic, and Orthodox Christian, Hindu, Jewish, and Muslim, met to sign a shared commitment against modern-day slavery; the declaration they signed calls for the elimination of slavery and human trafficking by the year 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Huffington_Post_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Huffington_Post-261">[261]</a></sup> The signatories were: <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mata_Amritanandamayi" title="Mata Amritanandamayi">Mātā Amṛtānandamayī</a>, Bhikkhuni Thich Nu <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2n_Kh%C3%B4ng" title="Chân Không">Chân Không</a> (representing Zen Master <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%ADch_Nh%E1%BA%A5t_H%E1%BA%A1nh" title="Thích Nhất Hạnh">Thích Nhất Hạnh</a>), Datuk K Sri Dhammaratana, Chief High Priest of Malaysia, Rabbi Abraham Skorka, Rabbi David Rosen, Abbas Abdalla Abbas Soliman, Undersecretary of State of Al Azhar Alsharif (representing Mohamed Ahmed El-Tayeb, Grand Imam of Al-Azhar), Grand Ayatollah Mohammad Taqi al-Modarresi, Sheikh Naziyah Razzaq Jaafar, Special advisor of Grand Ayatollah (representing Grand Ayatollah Sheikh Basheer Hussain al Najafi), Sheikh Omar Abboud, Justin Welby, Archbishop of Canterbury, and Metropolitan Emmanuel of France (representing Ecumenical Patriarch Bartholomew.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Huffington_Post_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Huffington_Post-261">[261]</a></sup></p>
<p>Groups such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Anti-Slavery_Group" title="American Anti-Slavery Group">American Anti-Slavery Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_International" title="Anti-Slavery International">Anti-Slavery International</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_the_Slaves" title="Free the Slaves">Free the Slaves</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_Society" title="Anti-Slavery Society">Anti-Slavery Society</a>, and the Norwegian Anti-Slavery Society continue to campaign to eliminate slavery.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics_2">Economics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="800" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg/220px-Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg/330px-Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg/440px-Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boulanger_Gustave_Clarence_Rudolphe_The_Slave_Market.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gustave_Boulanger" title="Gustave Boulanger">Gustave Boulanger</a>'s painting <i><a href="/wiki/The_Slave_Market" title="The Slave Market">The Slave Market</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Economists have attempted to model the circumstances under which slavery (and variants such as <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>) appear and disappear. One observation is that slavery becomes more desirable for landowners where land is abundant but labour is scarce, such that rent is depressed and paid workers can demand high wages. If the opposite holds true, then it becomes more costly for landowners to have guards for the slaves than to employ paid workers who can only demand low wages due to the amount of competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> Thus, first slavery and then serfdom gradually decreased in Europe as the population grew, but were reintroduced in the Americas and in Russia as large areas of new land with few people became available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> In his books, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_on_the_Cross:_The_Economics_of_American_Negro_Slavery" title="Time on the Cross: The Economics of American Negro Slavery">Time on the Cross</a></i> and <i>Without Consent or Contract: the Rise and Fall of American Slavery,</i> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fogel" title="Robert Fogel">Robert Fogel</a> maintains that slavery was in fact a profitable method of production, especially on bigger plantations growing cotton that fetched high prices in the world market. It gave whites in the South higher average incomes than those in the North, but most of the money was spent on buying slaves and plantations.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Juliao14.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="633" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Juliao14.JPG/170px-Juliao14.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Juliao14.JPG/255px-Juliao14.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Juliao14.JPG/340px-Juliao14.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Juliao14.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slave being whipped in Brazil, during the heyday of gold exploration in <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">Minas Gerais</a> (1770).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slavery is more common when the labour done is relatively simple and thus easy to supervise, such as large-scale growing of a single crop. It is much more difficult and costly to check that slaves are doing their best and with good quality when they are doing complex tasks. Therefore, slavery was seen as the most efficient method of production for large-scale crops like sugar and cotton, whose output was based on economies of scale. This enabled a <a href="/wiki/Gang_system" title="Gang system">gang system</a> of labour to be prominent on large plantations where field hands were monitored and worked with factory-like precision. Each work gang was based on an internal division of labour that not only assigned every member of the gang to a precise task but simultaneously made his or her performance dependent on the actions of the others. The hoe hands chopped out the weeds that surrounded the cotton plants as well as excessive sprouts. The plow gangs followed behind, stirring the soil near the rows of cotton plants and tossing it back around the plants. Thus, the gang system worked like an early version of the assembly line later to be found in factories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics since the 18th century have argued that slavery tends to retard technological advancement, since the focus is on increasing the number of slaves doing simple tasks rather than upgrading the efficiency of labour. Because of this, theoretical knowledge and learning in Greece—and later in Rome—was not applied to ease physical labour or improve manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> made the argument that free labour was economically better than slave labour, and argued further that slavery in Europe ended during the Middle Ages, and then only after both the church and state were separate, independent and strong institutions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> that it is nearly impossible to end slavery in a free, democratic and republican forms of governments since many of its legislators or political figures were slave owners, and would not punish themselves, and that slaves would be better able to gain their freedom when there was centralized government, or a central authority like a king or the church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> Similar arguments appear later in the works of <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Auguste Comte</a>, especially when it comes to Adam Smith's belief in the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> or what Comte called the "separation of the spiritual and the temporal" during the Middle Ages and the end of slavery, and Smith's criticism of masters, past and present. As Smith stated in the Lectures on Jurisprudence, "The great power of the clergy thus concurring with that of the king set the slaves at liberty. But it was absolutely necessary both that the authority of the king and of the clergy should be great. Where ever any one of these was wanting, slavery still continues.."</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="640" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg/220px-The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg/330px-The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg/440px-The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The inspection and sale of a slave</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slaves can be an attractive investment because the slave-owner only needs to pay for sustenance and enforcement. This is sometimes lower than the wage-cost of free labourers, because free workers earn more than sustenance, in these cases slaves have positive price. When the cost of sustenance and enforcement exceeds the wage rate, slave-owning would no longer be profitable, and owners would simply release their slaves. Slaves are thus a more attractive investment in high-wage environments, and environments where enforcement is cheap, and less attractive in environments where the wage-rate is low and enforcement is expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryan_Caplan_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bryan_Caplan-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>Free workers also earn <a href="/wiki/Compensating_differential" title="Compensating differential">compensating differentials</a>, whereby they are paid more for doing unpleasant work. Neither sustenance nor enforcement costs rise with the unpleasantness of the work, however, so slaves' costs do not rise by the same amount. As such, slaves are more attractive for unpleasant work, and less for pleasant work. Because the unpleasantness of the work is not internalised, being born by the slave rather than the owner, it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_externality" title="Negative externality">negative externality</a> and leads to over-use of slaves in these situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryan_Caplan_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bryan_Caplan-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>The weighted average global sales price of a slave is calculated to be approximately $340, with a high of $1,895 for the average trafficked sex slave, and a low of $40 to $50 for debt bondage slaves in part of Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhaliwal_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dhaliwal-36">[36]</a></sup> Worldwide slavery is a criminal offense but slave owners can get very high returns for their risk. According to researcher <a href="/wiki/Siddharth_Kara" title="Siddharth Kara">Siddharth Kara</a>, the profits generated worldwide by all forms of slavery in 2007 were $91.2 billion. That is second only to drug trafficking in terms of global criminal enterprises. The weighted average annual profits generated by a slave in 2007 was $3,175, with a low of an average $950 for bonded labour and $29,210 for a trafficked sex slave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhaliwal_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dhaliwal-36">[36]</a></sup> Approximately 40% of slave profits each year are generated by trafficked sex slaves, representing slightly more than 4% of the world's 29 million slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhaliwal_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dhaliwal-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Wright" title="Robert E. Wright">Robert E. Wright</a> has developed a <a href="/wiki/Conceptual_model" title="Conceptual model">model</a> that helps to predict when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firms" title="Firms">firms</a> (individuals, companies) will be more likely to use slaves rather than wage workers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured_servants" title="Indentured servants">indentured servants</a>, family members, or other types of <a href="/wiki/Laborer" title="Laborer">labourers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identification">Identification</span></h3>
<p>Throughout history, slaves were clothed in a distinctive fashion, particularly with respect to footwear or rather the lack thereof. This was both due to economic reasons as well as a distinguishing feature, especially in South Africa and South America. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a> slave code stated that <i>"Slaves must go barefoot and must carry passes."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCG_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-WCG-270">[270]</a></sup> This was the case in the majority of states that abolished slavery later in history, as most images from the respective historical period suggest that slaves were barefoot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slavery_brazil_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-slavery_brazil-271">[271]</a></sup> To quote Brother Riemer (1779): "[the slaves] are, even in their most beautiful suit, obliged to go barefoot. Slaves were forbidden to wear shoes. This was a prime mark of distinction between the free and the bonded and no exceptions were permitted." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_conditions_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-living_conditions-272">[272]</a></sup></p>
<p>As shoes have been considered badges of freedom since biblical times <i>"But the father said to his servants, Bring forth the best robe, and put [it] on him; and put a ring on his hand, and shoes on [his] feet (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#15:22" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 15:22</a>)"</i> this aspect has been an informal law wherever slavery existed. A barefoot person could therefore be clearly identified as a slave upon first sight. In certain societies this rule is valid to this day, as with the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> slavery which is still unofficially practiced, and their slaves have to go barefoot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sklaven_barfuss_bei_Tuareg_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sklaven_barfuss_bei_Tuareg-273">[273]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Apologies">Apologies</span></h2>
<p>On May 21, 2001, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_France" title="National Assembly of France">National Assembly of France</a> passed the <a href="/wiki/Christiane_Taubira" title="Christiane Taubira">Taubira</a> law, recognizing slavery as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crime_against_humanity" title="Crime against humanity">crime against humanity</a>. Apologies on behalf of African nations, for their role in trading their countrymen into slavery, remain an open issue since slavery was practiced in Africa even before the first Europeans arrived and the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> was performed with a high degree of involvement of several African societies. The black slave market was supplied by well-established slave trade networks controlled by local African societies and individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Indeed, as already mentioned in this article, slavery persists in several areas of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> until the present day.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>There is adequate evidence citing case after case of African control of segments of the trade. Several African nations such as the Calabar and other southern parts of Nigeria had economies depended solely on the trade. African peoples such as the Imbangala of Angola and the Nyamwezi of Tanzania would serve as middlemen or roving bands warring with other African nations to capture Africans for Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Several historians have made important contributions to the global understanding of the African side of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>. By arguing that African merchants determined the assemblage of trade goods accepted in exchange for slaves, many historians argue for African agency and ultimately a shared responsibility for the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1999, President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathieu_Kerekou" title="Mathieu Kerekou">Mathieu Kerekou</a> of <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> (formerly the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dahomey" title="Dahomey">Dahomey</a>) issued a national apology for the central role Africans played in the Atlantic slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apology_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-apology-277">[277]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luc_Gnacadja" title="Luc Gnacadja">Luc Gnacadja</a>, minister of environment and housing for Benin, later said: "The slave trade is a shame, and we do repent for it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dahomey_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-dahomey-278">[278]</a></sup> Researchers estimate that 3 million slaves were exported out of the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Coast" title="Slave Coast">Slave Coast</a> bordering the <a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Benin" title="Bight of Benin">Bight of Benin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dahomey_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-dahomey-278">[278]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Rawlings" title="Jerry Rawlings">Jerry Rawlings</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> also apologized for his country's involvement in the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apology_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-apology-277">[277]</a></sup></p>
<p>The issue of an apology is linked to <a href="/wiki/Reparations_for_slavery" title="Reparations for slavery">reparations for slavery</a> and is still being pursued by a number of entities across the world. For example, the Jamaican Reparations Movement approved its declaration and action Plan.</p>
<p>In September 2006, it was reported that the UK government might issue a "statement of regret" over slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> This was followed by a "public statement of sorrow" from <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> on November 27, 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> and a formal apology on March 14, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 25, 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Commonwealth of Virginia</a> resolved to 'profoundly regret' and apologize for its role in the institution of slavery. Unique and the first of its kind in the U. S., the apology was unanimously passed in both Houses as Virginia approached the 400th anniversary of the founding of <a href="/wiki/Jamestown,_Virginia" title="Jamestown, Virginia">Jamestown</a>, where the first slaves were imported into North America in 1619.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, which was a large slave trading port, apologized in 1999. On August 24, 2007, Mayor <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a> of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, United Kingdom, apologized publicly for Britain's role in colonial <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">slave trade</a>. "You can look across there to see the institutions that still have the benefit of the wealth they created from slavery," he said, pointing towards the financial district. He claimed that London was still tainted by the horrors of slavery. Specifically, London outfitted, financed, and insured many of the ships, which helped fund the building of London's docks. <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Jackson" title="Jesse Jackson">Jesse Jackson</a> praised Livingstone, and added that reparations should be made, one of his common arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jump_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-jump-283">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 30, 2008, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> passed a resolution apologizing for American slavery and subsequent discriminatory laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> In June 2009, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Senate" title="US Senate">US Senate</a> passed a resolution apologizing to African-Americans for the "fundamental injustice, cruelty, brutality, and inhumanity of slavery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup> The news was welcomed by President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, the nation's first President of African descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> Some of President Obama's ancestors were slave owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, Libyan leader <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> apologized for Arab involvement in the slave trade, saying: "I regret the behavior of the Arabs… They brought African children to North Africa, they made them slaves, they sold them like animals, and they took them as slaves and traded them in a shameful way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reparations">Reparations</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reparations_for_slavery" title="Reparations for slavery">Reparations for slavery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg"><img alt="refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1227" data-file-width="1980" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg/220px-Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg/330px-Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg/440px-Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_slaves_in_Zanzibar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monument to slaves in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There have been movements to achieve reparations for those formerly held as slaves, or sometimes their descendants. Claims for reparations for being held in slavery are handled as a <a href="/wiki/Private_law" title="Private law">civil law</a> matter in almost every country. This is often decried as a serious problem, since former slaves' relative lack of money means they often have limited access to a potentially expensive and futile <a href="/wiki/Service_of_process" title="Service of process">legal process</a>. Mandatory systems of fines and reparations paid to an as yet undetermined group of claimants from fines, paid by unspecified parties, and collected by authorities have been proposed by advocates to alleviate this "civil court problem.."Since in almost all cases there are no living ex-slaves or living ex-slave owners these movements have gained little traction. In nearly all cases the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judicial system</a> has ruled that the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_limitations" title="Statute of limitations">statute of limitations</a> on these possible claims has long since expired.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses_of_the_term">Other uses of the term</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>slavery</i> is often used as a pejorative to describe any activity in which one is coerced into performing.</p>
<p>Proponents of <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a> apply the term <i>slavery</i> to the condition of some or all human-owned animals, arguing that their status is comparable to that of human slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSpiegel_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSpiegel-289">[289]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some argue that <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">military drafts</a> and other forms of coerced government labour constitute state-operated slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-capitalism" title="Anarcho-capitalism">anarcho-capitalists</a> view government <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxation</a> as a form of slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antipsychiatry" title="Antipsychiatry">Antipsychiatry</a> proponents apply the term slavery to the involuntary psychiatric patient. There are no unbiased physical tests for mental illness, and the psychiatric patient must follow the orders of his/her psychiatrist. <a href="/wiki/Drapetomania" title="Drapetomania">Drapetomania</a> was a psychiatric diagnosis for a slave who did not want to be a slave. Thomas Szasz wrote a book titled "Psychiatric Slavery",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup> published in 1998 and a book titled " Liberation by Oppression: A Comparative Study of Slavery and Psychiatry",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> published in 2003.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wage_slavery">Wage slavery</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Labour_economics#Wage_slavery" title="Labour economics">Labour economics § Wage slavery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wage_labour" title="Wage labour">Wage labour</a></div>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialists" title="Socialists">socialists</a>, view <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">total and immediate wage dependence</a> as a form of slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> The labour market, as institutionalised under today's market economic systems, has been criticised,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> especially by both mainstream <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalists</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-English_Working_Class_p._599_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-English_Working_Class_p._599-297">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-English_Working_Class_p._912_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-English_Working_Class_p._912-298">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Ostergaard_p._133_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Ostergaard_p._133-299">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shop_Floor_p._37_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shop_Floor_p._37-300">[300]</a></sup> who utilise the term <a href="/wiki/Labour_economics#Wage_slavery" title="Labour economics">wage slavery</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merriam-webster.com_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-merriam-webster.com-301">[301]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup> as a <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a> for <a href="/wiki/Wage_labour" title="Wage labour">wage labour</a>. Socialists draw parallels between the trade of labour as a commodity and slavery. <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> is also known to have suggested such parallels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup></p>
<p>For Marxists, labour-as-commodity, which is how they regard wage labour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> provides an absolutely fundamental point of attack against capitalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> "It can be persuasively argued," noted one concerned philosopher<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup>, "that the conception of the worker's labour as a commodity confirms Marx's stigmatization of the wage system of private capitalism as 'wage-slavery;' that is, as an instrument of the capitalist's for reducing the worker's condition to that of a slave, if not below it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="No clue as to what this citation refers. Verifiable source required. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_film">In film</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_films_featuring_slavery" title="List of films featuring slavery">List of films featuring slavery</a></div>
<p>Film has been the most influential medium in the presentation of the history of slavery to the general public around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> The American film industry has had a complex relationship with slavery and until recent decades often avoided the topic. Films such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_of_a_Nation" title="Birth of a Nation">Birth of a Nation</a></i> (1915)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gone_with_the_Wind_(film)" title="Gone with the Wind (film)">Gone with the Wind</a></i> (1939) became controversial because they gave a favourable depiction. The last favourable treatment was <i><a href="/wiki/Song_of_the_South" title="Song of the South">Song of the South</a></i> from Disney in 1946. In 1940 <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_Trail_(film)" title="Santa Fe Trail (film)">The Santa Fe Trail</a></i> gave a liberal but ambiguous interpretation of <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(abolitionist)" title="John Brown (abolitionist)">John Brown</a>'s attacks on slavery—the film does not know what to do with slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup> The Civil Rights Movement in the 1950s made defiant slaves into heroes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> The question of slavery in American memory necessarily involves its depictions in feature films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spartacus_sheetA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="278" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Spartacus_sheetA.jpg/170px-Spartacus_sheetA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Spartacus_sheetA.jpg/255px-Spartacus_sheetA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Spartacus_sheetA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spartacus_sheetA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poster for the film <i><a href="/wiki/Spartacus_(film)" title="Spartacus (film)">Spartacus</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most Hollywood films used American settings, although <a href="/wiki/Spartacus_(film)" title="Spartacus (film)"><i>Spartacus</i> (1960)</a>, dealt with an actual revolt in the Roman Empire known as the <a href="/wiki/Third_Servile_War" title="Third Servile War">Third Servile War</a>. It failed and all the rebels were executed, but their spirit lived on according to the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Supper_(1976_film)" title="The Last Supper (1976 film)">The Last Supper</a></i> (<i>La última cena</i> in Spanish) was a 1976 film directed by Cuban <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1s_Guti%C3%A9rrez_Alea" title="Tomás Gutiérrez Alea">Tomás Gutiérrez Alea</a> about the teaching of Christianity to slaves in Cuba, and emphasizes the role of ritual and revolt. <i><a href="/wiki/Burn!" title="Burn!">Burn!</a></i> takes place on the imaginary Portuguese island of Queimada (where the locals speak Spanish) and it merges historical events that took place in Brazil, Cuba, Santo Domingo, Jamaica, and elsewhere. <i>Spartacus</i> stays surprisingly close to the historical record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians agree that films have largely shaped historical memories, but they debate issues of accuracy, plausibility, moralism, sensationalism, how facts are stretched in search of broader truths, and suitability for the classroom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup> Berlin argues that critics complain if the treatment emphasizes historical brutality, or if it glosses over the harshness to highlight the emotional impact of slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup></p>

<tr style="text-align: center; vertical-align: bottom;">
<td style="height:13px;">2006</td>
<td style="font-style: Italic"><a href="/wiki/Amazing_Grace_(2006_film)" title="Amazing Grace (2006 film)">Amazing Grace</a></td>

<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kolchin p. 96</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-223"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-223">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Berlin pp. 161–62</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Kolchin_pp._582-224"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kolchin_pp._582_224-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kolchin_pp._582_224-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Berlin pp. 168–69. Kolchin p. 96. Kolchin notes that Fogel and Engerman maintained that 84% of slaves moved with their families but "most other scholars assign far greater weight ... to slave sales. " Ransome (p. 582) notes that Fogel and Engermann based their conclusions on the study of some counties in Maryland in the 1830s and attempt to extrapolate that as reflective of the entire South over the entire period.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-davis2003-225"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-davis2003_225-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-davis2003_225-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Robert C. Thousand Oaks, CA: <a href="/wiki/SAGE_Publications" title="SAGE Publications">SAGE</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cato_Institute" title="Cato Institute">Cato Institute</a>. pp. 469–70. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.4135%2F9781412965811.n286" rel="nofollow">10.4135/9781412965811.n286</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4129-6580-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4129-6580-4">978-1-4129-6580-4</a>. <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Control_Number" title="Library of Congress Control Number">LCCN</a> <a class="external text" href="//lccn.loc.gov/2008009151" rel="nofollow">2008009151</a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/750831024" rel="nofollow">750831024</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASlavery&amp;rft.atitle=Slavery+in+America&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.aulast=Trenchard&amp;rft.btitle=The+Encyclopedia+of+Libertarianism&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DyxNgXs3TkJYC&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4135%2F9781412965811.n286&amp;rft_id=info%3Alccn%2F2008009151&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F750831024&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4129-6580-4&amp;rft.pages=469-70&amp;rft.place=Thousand+Oaks%2C+CA&amp;rft.pub=SAGE%3B+Cato+Institute&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>

<dt>Slavery in the modern era</dt>

<li>Jesse Sage and Liora Kasten, <i>Enslaved: True Stories of Modern Day Slavery</i>, Palgrave Macmillan, 2008 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781403974938">ISBN 978-1-4039-7493-8</a></li>

New Slavery in the Global Economy</i>, Revised Edition, <a href="/wiki/University_of_California_Press" title="University of California Press">University of California Press</a> 2004, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0520243846">ISBN 0-520-24384-6</a></li>

<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style=";"><span style="float:left;width:6em"> </span>
<div id="General_principles" style="font-size:114%">General principles</div>
</th>

<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li>Article 1: <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">Freedom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">Egalitarianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dignity" title="Dignity">Dignity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sibling" title="Sibling">Brotherhood</a></li>

<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"></div>
<table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0">
<tr>
<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style=";"><span style="float:left;width:6em"> </span>
<div id="International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" style="font-size:114%"><a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li>Articles 1 and 2: Right to <a href="/wiki/Freedom_from_discrimination" title="Freedom from discrimination">freedom from discrimination</a></li>
<li>Article 3: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_life" title="Right to life">Right to life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Security_of_person" title="Security of person">security of person</a></li>
<li>Article 4: Freedom from <strong class="selflink">slavery</strong></li>
<li>Article 5: Freedom from <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cruel_and_unusual_punishment" title="Cruel and unusual punishment">cruel and unusual punishment</a></li>
<li>Article 6: Right to <a href="/wiki/Person" title="Person">personhood</a></li>
<li>Article 7: <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">Equality before the law</a></li>
<li>Article 8: Right to effective <a href="/wiki/Legal_remedy" title="Legal remedy">remedy from the law</a></li>
<li>Article 9: Freedom from <a href="/wiki/Arbitrary_arrest_and_detention" title="Arbitrary arrest and detention">arbitrary arrest, detention</a> or <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">exile</a></li>
<li>Article 10: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_a_fair_trial" title="Right to a fair trial">Right to a fair trial</a></li>
<li>Article 11.1: <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">Presumption of innocence</a></li>
<li>Article 11.2: Prohibition of <a href="/wiki/Ex_post_facto_law" title="Ex post facto law">retrospective law</a></li>
<li>Article 12: Right to <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a></li>
<li>Article 13.1: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">Freedom of movement</a></li>
<li>Article 13.2: <a href="/wiki/Right_of_return" title="Right of return">Right of return</a></li>
<li>Article 14: <a href="/wiki/Right_of_asylum" title="Right of asylum">Right of asylum</a></li>
<li>Article 15: Right to a <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">nationality</a></li>
<li>Article 16: Right to <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">family</a> life</li>
<li>Article 17: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">Right to property</a></li>
<li>Article 18: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">Freedom of thought</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conscience" title="Conscience">conscience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">religion</a></li>
<li>Article 19: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">Freedom of opinion and expression</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_information" title="Freedom of information">information</a></li>
<li>Article 20.1: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_assembly" title="Freedom of assembly">Freedom of assembly</a></li>
<li>Article 20.2: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">Freedom of association</a></li>
<li>Article 21.1: Right to <a href="/wiki/Public_participation" title="Public participation">participation</a> in government</li>
<li>Article 21.2: Right of equal access to <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public office</a></li>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row">Article 30:</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><span class="wrap"><i>Nothing in this Declaration may be interpreted as implying for any State, group or person any right to engage in any activity or to perform any act aimed at the destruction of any of the rights and freedoms set forth herein.</i></span></li>
